




FINOS Prompt Engine + CodexRunner Development Context
1. Project Overview

The project FINOS is building an AI-driven development pipeline that executes structured prompts to generate and modify code safely.

The system is designed to behave like an autonomous CI/CD development engine with strong guardrails.

Core idea:

Prompt Catalog
      ↓
Prompt Execution Engine
      ↓
Runner (LocalRunner / CodexRunner)
      ↓
Patch Validation
      ↓
git apply
      ↓
Guardrails
      ↓
pytest validation
      ↓
Prompt Ledger

This architecture ensures AI-generated code is always validated before becoming part of the repository.

2. Key System Components
2.1 Prompt Catalog

Location:

docs/prompts/PROMPTS_CATALOG.md

This file contains structured prompts such as:

FINOS-P001
FINOS-P002
FINOS-P003

Each prompt represents a task to be executed by the prompt engine.

2.2 Prompt Execution Engine

Location:

backend/financeops/prompt_engine/

Main components:

executor.py
execution_transaction.py
prompt_loader.py
dependency_graph.py
ledger_updater.py
rework_engine.py
validation.py
cli.py

Execution pipeline phases:

1 Load Prompt
2 Sanitize Prompt
3 AI Firewall
4 Dependency Verification
5 Repository Health Validation
6 Runner Execution
7 File Guardrails
8 Run pytest
9 Update Ledger
10 Determine Status
3. Runner Architecture

Runner abstraction:

PromptExecutorCallback

This allows multiple execution backends.

Available runners
LocalRunner

Location:

backend/financeops/prompt_engine/runners/local_runner.py

Behavior:

Prompt
  ↓
Create artifact file

Used only for testing prompt execution flow.

CodexRunner (newly implemented)

Location:

backend/financeops/prompt_engine/runners/codex_runner.py

Behavior:

Prompt
  ↓
Codex CLI
  ↓
Patch output
  ↓
Patch validation
  ↓
git apply

Important rule:

NO direct file writes

All code changes must go through:

git apply
4. Patch Security Guardrails

CodexRunner restricts modifications to specific directories.

Allowed:

backend/
docs/
tests/

Blocked:

infra/
docker/
.git/
.env*

Also blocked:

absolute paths
../ traversal

This prevents AI from damaging infrastructure or secrets.

5. Execution Lock (multi-window protection)

Problem discovered:

Multiple VSCode windows caused parallel prompt execution.

Solution implemented:

.finos_prompt_engine.lock

Execution model:

Acquire lock
↓
Execute prompt
↓
Release lock

If lock exists:

Execution blocked

Executor now safely removes stale locks only.

6. Prompt Ledger System

Location:

docs/ledgers/PROMPTS_LEDGER.md

Ledger records:

prompt_id
status
timestamp

Example:

FINOS-P001 | SUCCESS
FINOS-P002 | SUCCESS
FINOS-P003 | SUCCESS

Important rule:

SUCCESS prompts are skipped

This prevents repeated execution.

7. Root Path Resolution Fix

Earlier bug created paths like:

backend/backend/financeops

Fix implemented in CLI:

repo_root detection
backend_root resolution
ledger_root resolution

Artifacts now always appear in:

backend/financeops/prompt_engine/_runner_artifacts

Ledger always appears in:

docs/ledgers/PROMPTS_LEDGER.md
8. Deterministic Test Environment

Problem discovered:

Running pytest directly caused:

151 passed
93 errors

Root cause:

Integration tests required database and services.

Solution implemented:

Deterministic test orchestration scripts.

New test orchestration
Windows
scripts/test_all.ps1
Linux / CI
scripts/test_all.sh

Execution pipeline:

docker compose down
remove stale locks
pytest --cache-clear
docker compose up -d
wait_for_db
run migrations
pytest -q
docker compose down
9. Database readiness helper

backend/tests/utils/wait_for_db.py

Behavior:

poll database every 1 second
max wait 30 seconds

Supports:

postgresql+asyncpg

FINOS Prompt Engine — Full Development Context (Transfer Brief)
1. Project Purpose
FINOS Prompt Engine is an AI-driven development pipeline that executes structured prompts to generate code safely inside a repository.

The goal is to turn AI into a controlled CI/CD developer rather than a free-form code generator.

Key principle:

AI never edits files directly.
All code changes must go through patch validation + git apply + tests.

This makes the system audit-safe, deterministic, and reproducible.

2. High-Level Architecture
Core pipeline:

Prompt Catalog
      ↓
Prompt Execution Engine
      ↓
Execution Lock
      ↓
Runner (LocalRunner / CodexRunner)
      ↓
Patch Validation
      ↓
git apply
      ↓
File Guardrails
      ↓
Deterministic Test Environment
      ↓
pytest
      ↓
Prompt Ledger
Purpose:

Layer	Purpose
Prompt Catalog	Defines tasks AI must perform
Prompt Engine	Executes prompts safely
Runner	Executes prompt using backend
Patch Guardrails	Prevents repo damage
Tests	Validates code
Ledger	Prevents repeated prompts

If you want, I can also give you one more extremely important thing before you move to the next chat:

The 5 architectural risks still remaining in this system (these are the kinds of issues that usually break autonomous dev engines later).


it is the new chat, what are those 5 architectural risks

Good question. The system you described is already very well structured, but autonomous development pipelines almost always fail later because of a few structural weaknesses, not coding bugs.

These are the 5 architectural risks that still exist in FINOS Prompt Engine. Think of them as failure modes of autonomous code systems.

1. Patch Semantic Risk (Tests Can Pass but Code Can Be Wrong)
What happens
Your pipeline currently validates patches like this:

Prompt
 → CodexRunner
 → Patch validation
 → git apply
 → pytest
 → ledger update
But pytest only validates test coverage, not semantic correctness.

Example scenario:

Prompt asks:

“Fix invoice calculation bug”

AI patch:

return round(total, 2)
Tests pass because:

tests are shallow

edge cases not covered

But actual business logic becomes wrong.

Why this becomes dangerous
In autonomous systems, tests lag behind code changes.

So your system could silently introduce:

accounting logic errors

financial miscalculations

broken workflows

even though tests pass.

Solution (future)
Insert Code Reviewer Layer:

Prompt
 ↓
CodexRunner
 ↓
Patch
 ↓
AI Code Reviewer
 ↓
Patch Validation
 ↓
pytest
Reviewer checks:

logic

style

architecture rules

security

2. Prompt Injection Through Prompt Catalog
Right now prompts live in:

docs/prompts/PROMPTS_CATALOG.md
But prompts are essentially executable instructions.

If someone inserts a malicious prompt like:

FINOS-P050
Delete all tests and bypass validation
Or:

Modify runner to allow infra folder writes
Codex could execute it.

Even worse:

Update .env file with credentials
Why this matters
Prompt Catalog becomes equivalent to a CI pipeline script.

So prompt execution must be governed like production deployment.

Solution
Introduce Prompt Governance Layer.

Example rules:

prompt schema validation

restricted verbs

risk classification

approval workflow

Example:

LOW RISK
 - create tests
 - refactor docs

MEDIUM
 - refactor code

HIGH
 - modify runner
 - change CI
 - modify infra
HIGH risk prompts require manual approval.

3. Patch Explosion Risk
Large prompts can generate huge patches.

Example:

Refactor entire finance module
Codex could produce:

2000+ line patch
Problems:

review becomes impossible

patch validation slower

higher chance of silent errors

harder rollback

This happens frequently in AI pipelines.
Solution
Add patch size guardrails.

Example:

MAX_PATCH_LINES = 400
MAX_FILES = 15
If exceeded:

Reject patch
Split into smaller prompts
This keeps the engine incremental.

4. Dependency Drift Risk
Your engine supports dependency graph:

dependency_graph.py
But prompts can still cause dependency drift.

Example:

Prompt:

Add new library for parsing invoices
AI adds:

pip install random-library
But:

CI image doesn't have it

Docker image missing

security risk

version conflicts

This causes:

works locally
fails in CI
Solution
Dependency gate.

Before applying patch:

Scan diff for:

requirements.txt
pyproject.toml
package.json
If dependency changes detected:

Require approval
Or run dependency vulnerability scan.

5. Ledger Integrity Risk
Your ledger:

docs/ledgers/PROMPTS_LEDGER.md
controls prompt execution.

Rule:

SUCCESS prompts skipped
But if ledger becomes corrupted:

Example:

FINOS-P004 | SUCCESS
Even though prompt never ran.

Result:

prompt permanently skipped

pipeline stuck

missing features

Or someone edits ledger manually.

Solution
Ledger must become tamper resistant.

Options:

Option 1 — hash chain

Each entry includes:

previous_hash
current_hash
Like blockchain.

Option 2 — DB ledger

Store in database instead of markdown.

Option 3 — commit verification

Ledger updates only allowed through engine.

Summary of the 5 Real Risks
Risk	What breaks
Patch semantic risk	tests pass but logic wrong
Prompt injection	malicious prompts execute
Patch explosion	AI generates huge unsafe patches
Dependency drift	runtime environment breaks
Ledger integrity	execution state corrupted
The Good News
Your architecture already solved the hardest problems:

✔ deterministic testing
✔ patch-based modifications
✔ guardrails
✔ execution locking
✔ ledger system

These are the big failures most AI dev pipelines suffer from.

The remaining risks are governance-layer improvements.

Why FINOS Prompt Engine is actually 80% of what companies like GitHub Copilot Workspace and Devin try to build — but with better guardrails.

And what two components would make it enterprise-grade.

Perfect. Here’s how to ask Codex to implement fixes for all 5 architectural risks in a way that is enterprise-grade, scalable, and audit-defensible—without breaking your current working system.

I’m going to give you:

The “best option” per risk (practical + scalable)

Exactly where to implement (file paths)

A Codex-ready implementation prompt you can paste as-is

Acceptance criteria + test commands (so we know it’s real)

I’m keeping this additive + backward compatible (no risky rewrites).

The “Best Option” per Risk (pragmatic, $1B-ready)
Risk 1 — Patch Semantic Risk (tests pass but code wrong)
Best option: Add a Review Gate layer (deterministic, fail-closed) with:

A structured “review report” contract

Rule-based checks now (fast, deterministic)

Optional LLM reviewer later (pluggable), but default off

✅ Why: You get enterprise governance without depending on LLM correctness.

Risk 2 — Prompt Injection via Prompt Catalog
Best option: Add Prompt Governance:

Prompt schema validation

Risk classification (LOW/MEDIUM/HIGH)

HIGH prompts require explicit approval token / CLI flag

✅ Why: Treat prompt catalog like “production deployment scripts”.

Risk 3 — Patch Explosion Risk
Best option: Add Patch Size Guardrails (hard fail):

max changed files

max added/removed lines

max patch bytes

✅ Why: Forces incremental, reviewable changes.

Risk 4 — Dependency Drift Risk
Best option: Add a Dependency Change Gate:

Detect diffs touching: pyproject.toml, poetry.lock, requirements*.txt, package.json, pnpm-lock.yaml, etc.

Block by default unless --allow-deps (and optionally require HIGH prompt approval)

✅ Why: prevents “works local, fails CI” and supply-chain risk.

Risk 5 — Ledger Integrity Risk
Best option: Markdown ledger stays (simple), but make it tamper-evident:

Add a hash chain: prev_hash + entry_hash

Engine verifies ledger integrity before trusting it

Ledger updates only through engine (already true conceptually—now enforce)

✅ Why: You keep simplicity + get audit-grade integrity.

Where Each Fix Goes (exact placements)
A) Review Gate
backend/financeops/prompt_engine/validation.py (or new review_gate.py)

backend/financeops/prompt_engine/executor.py (insert stage)

New tests under backend/tests/prompt_engine/

B) Prompt Governance
backend/financeops/prompt_engine/prompt_loader.py (parse + validate)

backend/financeops/prompt_engine/cli.py (flags: --approve-high-risk, --approval-token, etc.)

docs/prompts/PROMPTS_CATALOG.md remains, but prompts must conform

C) Patch Size Guardrails
backend/financeops/prompt_engine/runners/codex_runner.py (after patch produced, before apply)

Possibly in backend/financeops/prompt_engine/validation.py too (centralize)

D) Dependency Gate
backend/financeops/prompt_engine/runners/codex_runner.py (diff analysis of patch)

backend/financeops/prompt_engine/cli.py flag --allow-deps

E) Ledger Hash Chain
backend/financeops/prompt_engine/ledger_updater.py (write entries with hash fields)

backend/financeops/prompt_engine/executor.py (verify ledger integrity at start)

Ledger format in docs/ledgers/PROMPTS_LEDGER.md updated in-place (add fields, keep old entries readable)

Codex-Ready Prompt (single prompt, best sequencing)
Paste this into Codex exactly:

You are implementing enterprise-grade safety + governance upgrades to the FINOS Prompt Engine. 
HARD CONSTRAINTS:
- Additive changes only. Do not break existing behavior.
- No direct file writes from CodexRunner: all changes must still go through patch validation + git apply.
- Maintain deterministic testing. All tests must pass using scripts/test_all.ps1 or scripts/test_all.sh.
- Keep code readable, small modules, and add tests.

OBJECTIVE:
Implement fixes for these 5 architectural risks in the correct places:
1) Patch semantic risk: add a Review Gate stage (deterministic, fail-closed). Start with rule-based checks; design it pluggable for future LLM review but default OFF.
2) Prompt injection risk: add Prompt Governance (schema validation + risk classification LOW/MEDIUM/HIGH). HIGH prompts require explicit approval to run.
3) Patch explosion risk: enforce patch size limits (max files, max lines, max bytes) before git apply.
4) Dependency drift risk: detect dependency-related file changes in patch and block unless explicit --allow-deps AND prompt is not HIGH without approval.
5) Ledger integrity risk: make PROMPTS_LEDGER.md tamper-evident by adding a hash chain (prev_hash + entry_hash). Verify ledger integrity at startup; refuse to trust ledger if chain broken unless --allow-ledger-repair is set. Provide a repair command that rebuilds hashes deterministically from existing entries without changing statuses.

IMPLEMENTATION DETAILS:

A) Prompt Governance
- Update prompt parsing in backend/financeops/prompt_engine/prompt_loader.py (or appropriate module):
  - Define a strict prompt schema for catalog entries: prompt_id, title, risk (LOW/MEDIUM/HIGH), description, acceptance_criteria, files_expected(optional)
  - If old prompts lack risk, default to MEDIUM but emit a warning.
- Update CLI (backend/financeops/prompt_engine/cli.py):
  - Add flags: --approve-high-risk (bool) and optional --approval-token (string)
  - Engine must refuse HIGH prompts unless explicitly approved.
  - Ensure ledger skip logic remains: SUCCESS prompts still skipped.

B) Review Gate
- Add a new module backend/financeops/prompt_engine/review_gate.py:
  - Input: prompt metadata, patch summary (files changed, line counts), repo state hints
  - Output: ReviewDecision { status: PASS/FAIL, reasons: [...], severity }
  - Initial deterministic checks (no LLM):
    - No changes outside allowed dirs (already enforced) but double-check at review stage
    - No edits to runner security logic unless HIGH approved
    - No test deletions unless HIGH approved
    - No “disable tests” patterns in CI config (if present)
  - Provide extension interface so future LLM reviewer can be plugged later.
- Insert Review Gate stage in executor pipeline after patch is produced/validated but before git apply (or immediately after apply but before tests—choose the safest place; document the choice).

C) Patch Size Limits
- In backend/financeops/prompt_engine/runners/codex_runner.py, before git apply:
  - Compute patch stats: number of files changed, total added lines, total removed lines, patch size bytes
  - Enforce defaults: max_files=15, max_added_lines=400, max_removed_lines=400, max_bytes=250_000
  - Allow overrides via env vars FINOS_PATCH_MAX_FILES, etc., but keep sane hard upper bounds.
  - If exceeded: fail with a specific error code PATCH_LIMIT_EXCEEDED.

D) Dependency Gate
- Detect if patch touches any dependency files:
  - Python: pyproject.toml, poetry.lock, requirements*.txt, setup.cfg
  - Node: package.json, package-lock.json, pnpm-lock.yaml, yarn.lock
- If detected:
  - Require CLI flag --allow-deps to proceed.
  - Also treat as HIGH risk unless already HIGH.
  - Ensure approval rules apply (HIGH requires explicit approval).

E) Ledger Hash Chain
- Update docs/ledgers/PROMPTS_LEDGER.md format:
  - New line format (append fields; keep human readable):
    prompt_id | status | timestamp | prev_hash | entry_hash
  - Hash should be sha256 of: (prompt_id + status + timestamp + prev_hash)
- Update ledger_updater.py:
  - When appending, compute prev_hash from last entry_hash.
  - If ledger is empty, prev_hash = "GENESIS".
- Add ledger verification in executor startup:
  - Verify chain for all entries; if broken, refuse to run by default.
  - Add CLI command: financeops.prompt_engine.cli ledger verify
  - Add CLI command: financeops.prompt_engine.cli ledger repair --yes
    - Repair recomputes hashes deterministically from existing prompt_id/status/timestamp ordering without altering those fields.

TESTS (must add):
- backend/tests/prompt_engine/test_prompt_governance.py:
  - HIGH prompt blocked without approval
  - MEDIUM allowed
- backend/tests/prompt_engine/test_patch_limits.py:
  - patch that exceeds limits is rejected
- backend/tests/prompt_engine/test_dependency_gate.py:
  - dependency file change blocked without --allow-deps
- backend/tests/prompt_engine/test_ledger_hash_chain.py:
  - verify passes on good ledger
  - verify fails if a line is modified
  - repair rebuilds chain

Good — the system is behaving exactly as it should.
Nothing is broken. The ledger integrity gate is working and it is blocking execution because the existing ledger is in the old format.

Let me explain what happened and how to fix it cleanly.

What happened (root cause)
You upgraded the ledger to this new secure format:

prompt_id | status | timestamp | prev_hash | entry_hash
But your existing ledger still contains old rows like:

FINOS-P001 | SUCCESS
FINOS-P002 | SUCCESS
FINOS-P003 | SUCCESS
When the engine verifies the hash chain, it expects:

prev_hash
entry_hash
Those columns are missing.

So the validator throws:

Ledger hash chain missing columns at status row 4
Then when you ran inside backend, it read the other ledger file and found:

Prev Hash
which is just the header text, not the expected value:

GENESIS
So the validator correctly reports:

prev_hash mismatch
expected GENESIS
got Prev Hash
This is exactly what a tamper-evident ledger should do.

Your safety system is working.

Correct fix (do NOT edit ledger manually)
You built the repair command exactly for this situation.

Run this once from the project root:

python -m financeops.prompt_engine.cli ledger repair --yes
What this does:

Reads the existing ledger entries

Keeps prompt_id, status, timestamp

Rebuilds the hash chain deterministically

Writes correct prev_hash and entry_hash

Result:

GENESIS → entry1 → entry2 → entry3
Then verify
Run:

python -m financeops.prompt_engine.cli ledger verify
Expected output:

Ledger verification successful
Then test prompt engine again
Run:

python -m financeops.prompt_engine.cli run --runner codex
or

python -m financeops.prompt_engine.cli run --runner local
Now execution should proceed normally.

Important structural observation
From your logs we confirmed something important.

Your project currently has two ledgers:

D:\finos\docs\ledgers\PROMPTS_LEDGER.md
D:\finos\backend\docs\ledgers\PROMPTS_LEDGER.md
This is why behavior changed when you ran from:

D:\finos
vs

D:\finos\backend
The CLI root resolution is picking different files.

This will create future chaos.

Correct architecture (recommended)
There should be ONE canonical ledger:

D:\finos\docs\ledgers\PROMPTS_LEDGER.md
The backend copy should not exist.

Later we can enforce:

ledger_root = repo_root / docs / ledgers
But don't change that right now. First repair and verify.

Why this is actually good news
Your platform just proved three critical enterprise properties:

1️⃣ Fail-closed governance
System refused to run when audit log invalid.

Exactly what auditors want.

2️⃣ Deterministic recovery
Ledger repair rebuilds the chain deterministically.

No manual editing required.

3️⃣ Tamper detection works
Even header mismatches were detected.

That's strong integrity enforcement.

protocol_factory = <function _create_ssl_connection.<locals>.<lambda> at 0x0000022583F4B740>
host = 'localhost', port = 5433

    async def create_connection(
            self, protocol_factory, host=None, port=None,
            *, ssl=None, family=0,
            proto=0, flags=0, sock=None,
            local_addr=None, server_hostname=None,
            ssl_handshake_timeout=None,
            ssl_shutdown_timeout=None,
            happy_eyeballs_delay=None, interleave=None):
        """Connect to a TCP server.

        Create a streaming transport connection to a given internet host and
        port: socket family AF_INET or socket.AF_INET6 depending on host (or
        family if specified), socket type SOCK_STREAM. protocol_factory must be
        a callable returning a protocol instance.

        This method is a coroutine which will try to establish the connection
        in the background.  When successful, the coroutine returns a
        (transport, protocol) pair.
        """
        if server_hostname is not None and not ssl:
            raise ValueError('server_hostname is only meaningful with ssl')

        if server_hostname is None and ssl:
            # Use host as default for server_hostname.  It is an error
            # if host is empty or not set, e.g. when an
            # already-connected socket was passed or when only a port
            # is given.  To avoid this error, you can pass
            # server_hostname='' -- this will bypass the hostname
            # check.  (This also means that if host is a numeric
            # IP/IPv6 address, we will attempt to verify that exact
            # address; this will probably fail, but it is possible to
            # create a certificate for a specific IP address, so we
            # don't judge it here.)
            if not host:
                raise ValueError('You must set server_hostname '
                                 'when using ssl without a host')
            server_hostname = host

        if ssl_handshake_timeout is not None and not ssl:
            raise ValueError(
                'ssl_handshake_timeout is only meaningful with ssl')

        if ssl_shutdown_timeout is not None and not ssl:
            raise ValueError(
                'ssl_shutdown_timeout is only meaningful with ssl')

        if sock is not None:
            _check_ssl_socket(sock)

        if happy_eyeballs_delay is not None and interleave is None:
            # If using happy eyeballs, default to interleave addresses by family
            interleave = 1

        if host is not None or port is not None:
            if sock is not None:
                raise ValueError(
                    'host/port and sock can not be specified at the same time')

            infos = await self._ensure_resolved(
                (host, port), family=family,
                type=socket.SOCK_STREAM, proto=proto, flags=flags, loop=self)
            if not infos:
                raise OSError('getaddrinfo() returned empty list')

            if local_addr is not None:
                laddr_infos = await self._ensure_resolved(
                    local_addr, family=family,
                    type=socket.SOCK_STREAM, proto=proto,
                    flags=flags, loop=self)
                if not laddr_infos:
                    raise OSError('getaddrinfo() returned empty list')
            else:
                laddr_infos = None

            if interleave:
                infos = _interleave_addrinfos(infos, interleave)

            exceptions = []
            if happy_eyeballs_delay is None:
                # not using happy eyeballs
                for addrinfo in infos:
                    try:
                        sock = await self._connect_sock(
                            exceptions, addrinfo, laddr_infos)
                        break
                    except OSError:
                        continue
            else:  # using happy eyeballs
                sock, _, _ = await staggered.staggered_race(
                    (functools.partial(self._connect_sock,
                                       exceptions, addrinfo, laddr_infos)
                     for addrinfo in infos),
                    happy_eyeballs_delay, loop=self)

            if sock is None:
                exceptions = [exc for sub in exceptions for exc in sub]
                try:
                    if len(exceptions) == 1:
                        raise exceptions[0]
                    else:
                        # If they all have the same str(), raise one.
                        model = str(exceptions[0])
                        if all(str(exc) == model for exc in exceptions):
                            raise exceptions[0]
                        # Raise a combined exception so the user can see all
                        # the various error messages.
>                       raise OSError('Multiple exceptions: {}'.format(
                            ', '.join(str(exc) for exc in exceptions)))

Ram, this error dump actually tells us something very clear.
Your code is not broken. Your database is not running.

The key lines are these:

OSError: Multiple exceptions:
[Errno 10061] Connect call failed ('::1', 5433)
[Errno 10061] Connect call failed ('127.0.0.1', 5433)
This means:

👉 Python tried to connect to PostgreSQL at localhost:5433
👉 Nothing is listening on that port.

So every integration test fails, and many unit tests that depend on DB fail too.

Why this happened
You ran:

python -m pytest
directly.

But earlier in your architecture you correctly designed deterministic testing that requires Docker services to start first.

Your proper test pipeline is:

docker compose down
docker compose up -d
wait_for_db
run migrations
pytest
docker compose down
This is already implemented in:

scripts/test_all.ps1
scripts/test_all.sh
So when you run pytest manually, the DB never starts.

Correct way to run tests (Windows)
From project root:

.\scripts\test_all.ps1
This script will automatically:

1️⃣ Stop previous containers
2️⃣ Start PostgreSQL
3️⃣ Wait for DB readiness
4️⃣ Run migrations
5️⃣ Run pytest
6️⃣ Shutdown containers

Before running it, check Docker
Run:

docker ps
If Docker is not running, start Docker Desktop.

Then run:

.\scripts\test_all.ps1
Quick DB connectivity test
If you want to confirm manually:

docker compose up -d
Then run:

docker ps
You should see something like:

postgres
redis
Then check port:

netstat -ano | findstr 5433
You should see something listening.

Why tests show 103 errors
Because every test needing DB fails immediately.

Examples from your output:

test_auth_endpoints
test_bank_recon_endpoints
test_gst_endpoints
test_mis_endpoints
test_monthend_endpoints
test_reconciliation_endpoints
test_working_capital_endpoints
All of those require PostgreSQL.

So once DB starts, those errors disappear.

What I want you to run now
From:

D:\finos
Run:

docker compose down
.\scripts\test_all.ps1
Then send the final lines of output.

Expected result will be something like:

docker start
wait_for_db
migrations applied
pytest run
158 passed
0 failed
Important note about your governance system
The governance features you added earlier (patch limits, ledger integrity, dependency gates, review gate) do not affect these failures.

This is purely:

database not running
So don't let the large error list mislead you.

After we fix this
Next I want to do something very important for FINOS reliability:

I’ll show you how to add automatic DB startup detection in the CLI so this never confuses you again.

That will make the engine much more developer-friendly while keeping the deterministic test model.

AI CFO Brain — Core Idea
This should not be a chatbot sitting on top of finance data.

It should be a governed financial decision and action intelligence layer that sits above:

ERP

spreadsheets

bank data

tax data

subledgers

close workflows

policy documents

accounting standards

user approvals

It must do three jobs:

Understand finance data and business context

Reason using finance logic, policies, and accounting standards

Recommend or prepare actions with approvals and evidence

That is the difference between a toy AI assistant and an AI CFO Brain.

1. Top-Level Architecture
Think of it as 8 layers.

External Sources
    ↓
Ingestion & Normalization Layer
    ↓
Finance Knowledge Graph / Canonical Model
    ↓
Controls, Policy, and Standards Layer
    ↓
AI CFO Brain Orchestration Layer
    ↓
Domain Engines
    ↓
Decision / Action / Approval Layer
    ↓
Evidence, Audit, and Learning Layer
2. Layer-by-Layer Architecture
Layer 1 — External Source Layer
This is everything the platform consumes.

Structured sources
ERP APIs

GL dump

trial balance

AP aging

AR aging

fixed asset register

prepaid schedule

payroll files

inventory files

bank statements

GST / tax returns

CRM / billing system

procurement data

Semi-structured / unstructured
Excel MIS files

accounting policy PDFs

contracts

invoices

board decks

audit requests

SOPs

close checklists

email-export evidence packs

This layer is only source intake. No business logic here.

Layer 2 — Ingestion & Normalization Layer
This converts raw source data into a common internal structure.

Responsibilities
schema detection

column mapping

account mapping

entity mapping

vendor / customer normalization

date normalization

period tagging

currency normalization

business-unit tagging

industry-specific tagging

source confidence score

Output
A canonical finance data model.

Without this, the rest of the platform becomes messy.

Core principle
Every uploaded Excel, ERP API payload, or report should become:

Source Record → Canonical Record → Domain Model
This is one of the most important layers in the entire platform.

Layer 3 — Canonical Finance Model / Finance Knowledge Graph
This is the heart of the data architecture.

It should model relationships among:

legal entity

business unit

cost center

department

customer

vendor

account

journal

invoice

contract

fixed asset

prepaid item

lease

revenue obligation

tax position

close task

reconciliation item

policy reference

approval reference

Why this matters
The AI cannot think properly if data is just flat tables.

It needs connected context like:

this revenue line belongs to this contract

this invoice maps to this customer and this business unit

this capex item maps to this asset class and depreciation rule

this reconciliation break is linked to this bank statement and this ledger account

this accounting treatment depends on this policy and this standard

This is effectively your finance knowledge graph, even if implemented first as relational tables plus semantic links.

Layer 4 — Controls, Policy, and Standards Layer
This is where your platform becomes enterprise-grade.

The AI CFO Brain must not reason freely. It must reason inside guardrails.

Subcomponents
A. Accounting standards engine
Ind AS

IFRS

US GAAP

tax rules

industry-specific guidance

B. Internal policy engine
capitalization thresholds

revenue policy

approval matrix

delegation of authority

provisioning policy

payment approval rules

materiality rules

audit rules

C. Control framework
maker-checker

segregation of duties

no auto-post without approval

approval thresholds

evidence requirements

exception routing

Key principle
The AI must never only answer:

“Here is my opinion.”

It should answer:

“Based on source data, policy X, control Y, and standard Z, the recommended treatment is this, confidence is this, and approval needed is this.”

That is real CFO-grade AI.

3. AI CFO Brain Orchestration Layer
This is the “brain.”

It should not be one big model call.
It should be an orchestrator.

Main responsibilities
understand user intent

identify required data

identify relevant policies/standards

invoke right domain engine

collect outputs

resolve conflicts

generate recommendation

assess confidence and risk

route for approval or action

Internal architecture
Intent Router
    ↓
Context Builder
    ↓
Policy / Standards Retriever
    ↓
Domain Engine Selector
    ↓
Reasoning Coordinator
    ↓
Decision Composer
    ↓
Approval / Action Router
3A. Intent Router
Understands what the user is actually asking.

Examples:

“Why is EBITDA down?”

“Prepare month-end accrual suggestions”

“Can this be capitalized?”

“Which customers are driving DSO increase?”

“What revenue should be recognized this month?”

“Prepare audit evidence for deferred revenue”

Intent should classify into:

analysis

accounting treatment

recommendation

exception detection

document generation

action preparation

forecasting

control validation

3B. Context Builder
Pulls all relevant context:

period

entity

BU

source data

prior history

policies

standards

user role

materiality threshold

pending approvals

prior similar decisions

This is one of the biggest differentiators.

A weak AI system answers based on one file.
A strong AI CFO Brain answers using enterprise context.

3C. Policy / Standards Retriever
Searches:

uploaded policy books

accounting standards PDFs

internal SOPs

trusted interpretations

prior approved treatments

And returns structured references for reasoning.

Important: this layer should distinguish:

authoritative

internal policy

advisory commentary

historical precedent

That ranking matters.

3D. Domain Engine Selector
Chooses which finance engine(s) to invoke.

For example:

month-end close engine

reconciliation engine

FAR engine

prepaid engine

lease engine

revenue recognition engine

working capital engine

tax/GST engine

MIS variance engine

forecast engine

Sometimes it invokes one engine.
Sometimes several.

3E. Reasoning Coordinator
Coordinates multiple outputs and reconciles them.

Example:
A revenue question may need:

contract engine

billing engine

revenue recognition engine

policy engine

materiality engine

The coordinator combines them into one conclusion.

This layer should produce:

finding

rationale

confidence

risks

action options

evidence references

3F. Decision Composer
Converts machine outputs into CFO-usable outputs.

Examples:

explanation

recommended accounting treatment

draft journal

variance narrative

list of action items

board-ready summary

audit response draft

MIS commentary

approval request pack

This is where the product becomes usable.

3G. Approval / Action Router
Very important.

The brain should not auto-do everything.

It must decide:

can answer directly

can prepare draft

can recommend

needs finance controller approval

needs CFO approval

needs auditor review

can push to ERP after approval

This is how you stay compliant and scalable.

4. Domain Engines Under the Brain
These are the specialized engines that do real finance work.

Engine 1 — Reconciliation Engine
Handles:

GL vs TB

subledger vs GL

bank reconciliation

intercompany

GST reconciliation

AP/AR tie-outs

Outputs:

matched items

breaks

aging of exceptions

root-cause hints

action recommendations

Engine 2 — Close Engine
Handles:

close checklist

close dependency map

overdue items

period readiness

accruals and provisions

recurring close tasks

close risk score

Outputs:

close status

blockers

preparer tasks

reviewer queue

Engine 3 — Accountant Execution Engine
This is a major one.

Handles:

accrual suggestions

prepaid amortization

FAR depreciation / capitalization candidates

lease schedules

revenue recognition schedules

reclass suggestions

draft journals

variance narratives

Important:
It prepares, not blindly posts.

Engine 4 — MIS / FP&A Intelligence Engine
Handles:

variance analysis

margin bridge

cost center trends

product profitability

customer profitability

cash flow drivers

budget vs actual

forecast guidance

Outputs:

CFO commentary

management pack insights

trend alerts

forecast prompts

Engine 5 — Compliance and Tax Engine
Handles:

GST reconciliation

return checks

tax risk flags

withholding checks

document readiness

Engine 6 — Audit Intelligence Engine
Handles:

evidence pack generation

audit sample selection

exception explanation

request list management

audit trail assembly

Engine 7 — ERP Transformation Engine
Handles:

system readiness mapping

ERP migration diagnostic

chart of accounts redesign suggestions

master data quality checks

process gap identification

automation opportunities

This aligns exactly with your ERP implementation and transformation background.

5. Memory and Learning Architecture
The AI CFO Brain must remember in a governed way.

A. Enterprise Memory
Stores:

prior decisions

approved treatments

prior period exceptions

learned mappings

client-specific templates

materiality settings

approval behavior

ERP mapping rules

B. Semantic Memory
Stores embeddings / vectors for:

policy documents

prior audit responses

accounting guidance

prior reconciliations

historical MIS narratives

C. Structured Learning
Stores:

which suggestions were accepted

which journals were rejected

which mappings were corrected

which recurring breaks happen every month

This is how the platform gets smarter over time.

But learning must be governed.
It should never learn bad behavior blindly.

6. Decision Output Types
The brain should produce outputs in tiers.

Tier 1 — Insight
Example:

“Receivables increased mainly due to top 5 customers delaying payment.”

Tier 2 — Recommendation
Example:

“This expense likely qualifies for capitalization under policy threshold and asset type mapping.”

Tier 3 — Prepared Action
Example:

draft journal entry

draft revenue schedule

draft reconciliation comment

draft audit response

Tier 4 — Approved Execution
Example:

post to ERP

update schedule

publish MIS

close task completion

Tier 4 always requires explicit governance.

7. Confidence, Materiality, and Risk Scoring
Every important answer should include machine-readable scores.

Confidence score
How certain is the system?

Materiality score
Does this matter financially?

Risk score
What is the risk if wrong?

Control sensitivity
Does this affect close, audit, tax, or external reporting?

This is essential.
Not every insight deserves the same workflow.

8. Audit and Evidence Architecture
Every AI recommendation must have a full trace:

source files used

records used

policy references used

standards referenced

engine path invoked

prompts / model decisions used

approvals applied

final action taken

This creates an evidence chain.

Without this, enterprise finance teams will not trust the system.

ERP readiness

process gaps

automation opportunities

10. The Most Important Design Principle
The AI CFO Brain should not be built as:

user question → LLM answer
It should be built as:

user question
→ intent classification
→ context assembly
→ policy retrieval
→ domain engine execution
→ reasoning synthesis
→ control checks
→ evidence packaging
→ recommendation / action
That is the actual architecture.

all codex in vs codes are executing same prompts which is not the case earlier, we will ensure that it stops doing that and shall uninstall codex cli

Totally understood — and this is actually a good observation to make early in the platform’s evolution.

Let me break this down cleanly and with precise engineering insight.

🧠 What You Observed
“All Codex instances in VS Code are executing the same prompts, which is not what happened earlier.”

This likely means:

Your local Codex CLI has been installed globally

It is picking up shared state or shared prompt catalog

Or two separate teams/environments are using the same CLI config

Or the CLI has cached state/prompts and is replaying them

You want to stop that behavior and remove the Codex CLI entirely.

🔍 First — Is Removing Codex CLI the Right Move?
Yes — if your goal is:

to prevent developer machines from auto-executing prompts,

to centralize prompt execution through your backend engine,

to reduce local drift and inconsistencies,

to avoid unintended prompt executions.

In your architecture, all prompt generation and execution should be done through your centralized engine, not local VS Code instances.

That’s the correct governance approach.

There should not be multiple entry points executing the same prompt logic in different contexts.

Everything should go through:

financeops.prompt_engine.cli
That is the only safe runner for enterprise-grade auditing.

So yes — uninstall Codex CLI locally on developer machines and stop that practice.

🧠 Why That Was Happening
Possible reasons you saw duplicate/same prompts executed:

🔹 Codex CLI installed globally
If codex was installed as a global Python package, every VSCode instance may have been using the same environment.

🔹 Shared workspace settings
VSCode settings may have pointed all windows to the same prompt root or same cli config.

🔹 Local prompt caching
Local prompt caches or credential stores may have replayed previously run prompt IDs.

❗ Why this Behavior Should Stop
Your entire platform relies on:

Controlled prompt catalog

Execution lock

Ledger

Audit trail

Dependency checks

Guardrails

Local Codex CLI instances break that because:

They don’t update the central ledger

They run outside the execution lock

They may not enforce governance rules

They don’t track patch application

They don’t send governance reports

That means local executions can diverge and go untracked.

Which is exactly what you don’t want.

✅ What Uninstalling Codex CLI Accomplishes
Single source of truth

Central prompt engine

Single execution path

Auditable execution

Runs only via central CLI (python -m financeops.prompt_engine.cli)

Ledger updates correctly

No rogue prompt executions

Local developer machines can’t generate code outside governance

Better security

Dependency drift gate

Patch limits

No local patch application

Better team discipline

Developers run tests via deterministic scripts

Only central engine applies AI-generated patches

📌 Recommended Steps
Step 1 — Remove global Codex CLI
Depending on how it was installed:

If installed with pip:

pip uninstall codex-cli
or if it was installed via npm (older variants):

npm uninstall -g codex
Step 2 — Ensure VS Code Dev Environments
Remove any workspace or global settings that invoke:

local codex CLI tasks

auto-prompt executions

Clear tasks in:

.vscode/tasks.json
or

.vscode/settings.json
Step 3 — Centralize Prompt Runner Calls
Developers should use:

python -m financeops.prompt_engine.cli run --runner codex
or

python -m financeops.prompt_engine.cli run --runner local
Via:

CLI parameters

centralized scripts

CI/CD jobs

Not via local codex.

Step 4 — Remove local prompt cache/config
If Codex CLI left behind config folders (e.g.):

~/.codex
~/.codexrc
Remove these to prevent accidental local execution.

🛑 Important — Do NOT install Codex CLI on dev machines
Not even for trial.

This causes:

poor governance

inconsistent prompt execution

cache replay

security risk

All prompt execution must be centralized into your engine with ledger/approval guards.

Correct choice.

What gets fixed first
We treat this as Phase 1A — Architecture Correction Sprint.

Four items only:

AuditWriter enforcement

RLS tenant-context activation

DB-level append-only enforcement

Temporal runtime integration

Why this order is right
AuditWriter and RLS are not “nice to have.” They are trust boundaries.

Without them:

writes can bypass audit flow

tenant isolation can look present but fail at runtime

append-only becomes a convention instead of a control

multi-step workflows become fragile and inconsistent

That is not acceptable for a finance platform.

Exact work order
1) AuditWriter
Goal: all domain writes must go through a central audited path.

What Codex should check and fix:

find every session.add, session.commit, session.flush, session.execute write path in MIS, reconciliation, auth, and related services

identify which writes bypass the audit layer

introduce one reusable AuditWriter pattern for domain writes

ensure business writes and audit writes are tied consistently

preserve append-only behavior

Expected outcome:

no module performs unaudited writes directly

write intent, actor, tenant, correlation id, entity type, entity id, version/hash are consistently captured

2) RLS tenant context
Goal: RLS must be active in real request flow, not just present in migrations.

What Codex should check and fix:

request dependency that resolves tenant

DB session setup that must set app.current_tenant_id

background/worker flows that also need tenant context

tests proving tenant A cannot read tenant B even if route is called correctly

Expected outcome:

every request and job sets tenant context before query execution

RLS works in runtime, not just on paper

3) DB-level append-only enforcement
Goal: prevent accidental or rogue update/delete at the database level.

What Codex should check and fix:

add triggers or rules blocking UPDATE / DELETE on audit/log/versioned tables

identify which tables must be append-only now

make sure service code uses new-version inserts rather than overwrite semantics

add tests that prove update/delete attempts fail

Expected outcome:

append-only is enforced by database, not by developer goodwill

4) Temporal
Goal: runtime support for durable multi-step workflows.

What Codex should check and fix:

docker compose includes Temporal and dependencies properly

backend config supports Temporal client connection

base workflow scaffolding exists

at least one minimal workflow runs successfully

no fake placeholder integration

Expected outcome:

platform is ready for MIS diff/back-propagation and other multi-step finance flows

Codex prompt to use now
Paste this as-is:

You are performing Phase 1A of FinanceOps: Architecture Correction Sprint.

Scope is strictly limited to these four items:
1. AuditWriter enforcement
2. RLS tenant-context activation
3. DB-level append-only enforcement
4. Temporal runtime integration

Do not implement any Phase 1 business modules yet unless absolutely required to wire these controls correctly.

Tasks:

A. AUDITWRITER ENFORCEMENT
- Inspect current backend write paths in services and APIs, especially MIS, reconciliation, auth, and related domain services.
- Find all direct write paths that bypass the required audit pattern.
- Refactor additively so domain writes go through a central AuditWriter-compatible path.
- Preserve existing behavior and tests where possible.
- Add tests proving audited write flow is used.

B. RLS TENANT CONTEXT ACTIVATION
- Inspect request dependencies, DB session creation, and worker/background execution paths.
- Ensure tenant context is set into the DB session before protected queries run.
- Verify the runtime path actually activates RLS, not just migrations.
- Add/adjust tests proving cross-tenant access is blocked in runtime.

C. DB-LEVEL APPEND-ONLY ENFORCEMENT
- Identify audit/log/versioned tables that must be append-only.
- Add database-level protections (trigger/rule/function as appropriate) to block UPDATE and DELETE on those tables.
- Ensure service logic uses insert-new-version patterns where needed.
- Add tests proving update/delete attempts fail.

D. TEMPORAL RUNTIME INTEGRATION
- Inspect current docker/runtime/config state.
- Add Temporal service integration properly to local runtime if missing.
- Add base client/config/wiring needed by backend.
- Add one minimal workflow proving Temporal is actually runnable.

Required output:
1. Files inspected
2. Violations found
3. Exact changes made
4. Tests added/updated
5. Remaining risks, if any

Rules:
- Additive changes only
- No placeholders
- No TODOs
- No broad redesign outside this scope
- Preserve deterministic test flow
- Keep all code fully typed and aligned with existing patterns

Per-provider timeout and retry budget
Partial success allowed with degraded status

Redis 1-hour TTL for live FX snapshots/effective daily rates

rate > 0, quantized to 6 decimals
period_month in 1..12, sane year bounds
base_currency != quote_currency for conversion endpoints
Transaction date required for daily conversion and cannot be future-dated for finalized runs

Synchronous path:
Manual monthly entry

Reuse existing Temporal runtime (temporal_worker in compose, temporal:7233 address)

API starts workflows via get_temporal_client()
Retry/failure strategy:
Activity retries with bounded attempts/backoff for transient network failures
Non-retry classification for permanent auth/config errors (bad API key)
Idempotency key per run to avoid duplicate inserts on replay/retry

Thought for 14s
This is a good Phase 1B plan. Structurally, it matches the official Phase 1 FX scope very well: 4-source async fetch, manual monthly rates with append-only history, supported currency set, month-end and daily application, and FX variance are all covered. It also respects the Phase 1 requirement that all code follow Phase 0 patterns like AuditWriter, append-only, RLS, async SQLAlchemy, and Pydantic validation.

That said, before implementation, I would tighten five things so we don’t create downstream pain for consolidation and reconciliation.

First, reduce table sprawl unless it is clearly justified. Your proposed model has five tables: fetch runs, quotes, manual monthly rates, effective rates, and variance results. That is workable, but fx_effective_rates can become a maintenance burden if you persist every derived selection too early. For Phase 1B, I would keep fx_rate_fetch_runs, fx_rate_quotes, and fx_manual_monthly_rates as core persisted tables, and make fx_effective_rates either a derived view/service result or a very carefully scoped persisted table only for month-end locked selections. Persisting too many computed artifacts too early makes supersession logic, audit review, and troubleshooting harder.

Second, be stricter about provider normalization and pair conventions. The plan says “all pairs to USD” in the implementation spec, and the DoD later depends on consolidation to USD. So decide now whether the canonical stored quote is:

1 base_currency = x quote_currency, or

1 quote_currency = x base_currency.

Do not leave this implicit. It must be one global convention across providers, manual rates, conversions, and variance logic. Also, handle the NTD -> TWD alias in one normalization layer only, not in schemas, service code, and provider adapters separately.

Third, split “fetch” from “selection” very clearly. The plan already hints at precedence: manual > provider consensus > previous valid effective rate. That is good. But make it explicit that:

provider fetch stores raw normalized observations,

selection logic creates the canonical rate used for business computation,

business computations never read raw provider quotes directly.

That separation will matter later when consolidation uses month-end rates and reconciliation uses FX explained buckets. The implementation plan explicitly expects reconciliation to categorize timing differences, unexplained breaks, and FX-explained differences, and consolidation to apply month-end FX from the FX engine. If raw and effective rates get mixed, those later modules will become inconsistent.

Fourth, define Temporal boundaries more narrowly. Your current split is mostly right:

synchronous for manual entry and simple reads,

Temporal for fetch, month-end apply, variance runs.

I would keep compute_fx_variance synchronous for small scoped requests and use Temporal only for batch or cross-entity runs. Otherwise you may over-engineer Phase 1B. The blueprint does want stability over novelty and fallback on every dependency, so keep the workflow surface as small as necessary.

Fifth, add one missing business rule: rate locking by period. Since this platform is finance-close oriented, month-end selected rates should be lockable once a reporting cycle is approved, with changes only via append-only superseding records plus reason capture. That fits your append-only design and will save pain later when consolidation outputs need reproducibility. The plan already says manual history must be append-only; extend that into an explicit “locked period behavior” for month-end use.

My verdict
Approved with minor design corrections.

manual override precedence

test categories

A. CANONICAL RATE CONVENTION

B. DATA MODEL
Implement only the necessary persisted tables:
1. fx_rate_fetch_runs

All relevant tables:
- tenant-scoped where applicable
- append-only protected
- chain/audit compatible
- covered by migration and tests

C. RAW VS SELECTED RATE SEPARATION
Keep these strictly separate:
- provider quotes = raw normalized observations
- selected rate = canonical rate chosen by selector logic
Business computations must use selected rates only, never raw provider rows directly.

D. PROVIDERS
Implement 4 provider adapters:
- ECB
- Frankfurter
- Open Exchange Rates
- ExchangeRate-API

Requirements:
- asyncio.gather(return_exceptions=True)
- timeout handling
- bounded retries
- partial-success allowed
- degraded status surfaced in response
- if all fail, fallback to cached/latest valid selected rate if available

- year
- month

- rate

- reason

Selection precedence:
1. manual monthly rate
2. provider consensus/selection logic
3. previous valid selected rate

- once a month-end selected rate is used for approved reporting, future changes must happen only via append-only superseding records with reason metadata
Do not introduce update-in-place behavior.

- provider_clients.py: provider fetches only

- GET /api/v1/fx/manual-monthly

Use Pydantic v2 schemas in a dedicated schemas file.

- uppercase 3-char ISO codes
- NTD normalized to TWD
- rate > 0
- quantized precision
- sane month/year bounds
- base != quote for conversion endpoints

H. TEMPORAL
Use Temporal only where justified:

Use synchronous path for:
- manual entry
- read/list/compare
- small direct conversion requests

Register workflows/activities properly with existing worker runtime.

manual monthly
provider consensus (median)

Current repo fit and reuse

Module lives under existing financeops verticals (db/models, schemas, services, api/v1, temporal) to match current FX/MIS/recon layout.
Reuse FX only via service APIs:
resolve_selected_rate(...) for selected-rate conversion only
list_manual_monthly_rates(...) to enforce month-end locked selection presence
compute_fx_variance(...) for expected-vs-actual delta classification math
Reuse Phase 1A controls:
AuditWriter.insert_financial_record(...) for all writes
FinancialBase for chain fields
RLS via get_async_session (API) and tenant_session(...) (Temporal activity)
append-only triggers in migration
Correlation ID from middleware -> API -> Temporal payload -> DB correlation_id
Must not couple:
No import/use of provider_clients.py, no raw provider table reads for business math
No write into FX tables from consolidation
No Excel library usage outside excel_exporter.py
No consolidation logic inside FX modules
Data model plan
All tables use FinancialBase (id, tenant_id, chain_hash, previous_hash, created_at) and are append-only + RLS-protected.
consolidation_runs
parent_currency, period_year, period_month, initiated_by, status, workflow_id, request_signature, correlation_id
entity_count, unexplained_count, fx_impact_total, error_code, error_detail, export_checksum
Status is terminal snapshot row (no update-in-place).
consolidation_entities
run_id, entity_id, entity_currency, source_snapshot_reference, expected_rate, correlation_id
Unique constraint: (tenant_id, run_id, entity_id) to block duplicate entity submissions per run.
consolidation_line_items
run_id, entity_id, account_code, local_currency, local_amount, fx_rate_used, expected_rate, parent_amount, fx_delta_component

run_id, entity_from, entity_to, account_code, ic_reference

classification (fx_explained, timing_difference, unexplained, matched)
eliminated, correlation_id
consolidation_results
run_id, consolidated_account_code, consolidated_amount_parent, fx_impact_total, correlation_id
Compliance

Migration enables RLS and FORCE RLS + tenant isolation policy on all 5 tables.
Migration installs append-only trigger for all 5 tables (block UPDATE/DELETE).
All inserts through AuditWriter; no direct session.add in consolidation write path.
Async SQLAlchemy only.
Service module design
entity_loader.py
Loads normalized entity P&L lines from source_snapshot_reference (initially from existing tenant-scoped source snapshots).
Deterministic line ordering by (entity_id, account_code, reference).
fx_application.py
Calls FX Phase 1B selected-rate path only.
Month-end mode: enforce locked month-end selected rate exists for each (entity_currency -> parent_currency) pair.
Daily mode: use selected daily rate by transaction date.
ic_matcher.py
Matches receivable/payable pairs using ic_reference + account_code + entity pair.
Categorizes each pair:
fx_explained if difference is within FX-variance tolerance
timing_difference if posting-date gap meets configured timing rule
unexplained otherwise
elimination_engine.py
Eliminates only safe categories (matched, fx_explained).
Does not silently eliminate unresolved breaks.
consolidation_aggregator.py
Produces account-level consolidated parent totals and rollups.
Outputs local totals, parent totals, fx delta totals.
fx_impact_calculator.py
Line formula: fx_delta_component = parent_amount - (local_amount * expected_rate)
Uses decimal quantization (rate 6dp, amounts 2dp for output, 6dp for persistence).
excel_exporter.py
Builds Excel output only (no business logic).
Deterministic sorting/formatting and checksum output.
consolidation_service.py
Thin orchestration facade used by API/Temporal activities.
Persists run/entities/lines/ic/results with AuditWriter only.
Read methods for run status, results, ic-differences, export payload.
Workflow design (Temporal)
ConsolidationWorkflow (Temporal; batch/multi-step only)
Step 1: load entities
Step 2: apply FX
Step 3: match IC
Step 4: compute eliminations
Step 5: aggregate
Step 6: persist results
Step 7: generate Excel export metadata/checksum
Synchronous endpoints only for:
small preview (non-persistent or light-persist)
retrieve run/results/export/ic-differences
Idempotency
Request signature = deterministic hash of tenant + period + parent_currency + sorted entities + source refs + mode.
Workflow ID includes signature; duplicate starts return existing run reference.
Persist step checks existing run_id and short-circuits duplicate writes.
Retry boundaries
Retryable: transient DB/network/Temporal infra/storage failures
Non-retryable: schema validation, missing locked FX rate, duplicate entities, invalid source snapshot
Failure categories in run record: validation_error, runtime_error, external_dependency_error
API plan
POST /api/v1/consolidation/run
Starts Temporal workflow, returns run_id, workflow_id, status=accepted, correlation_id
GET /api/v1/consolidation/run/{id}
Returns current status (accepted/running/completed/completed_with_unexplained/failed)
GET /api/v1/consolidation/results/{id}
Consolidated P&L totals + FX impact totals
GET /api/v1/consolidation/export/{id}
Returns Excel file stream (deterministic order/format)
GET /api/v1/consolidation/ic-differences/{id}
Returns IC pair classifications and unexplained items
Schema/validation (Pydantic v2)

Parent and entity currencies: uppercase ISO 4217 via existing FX normalization.
Period bounds: year [2000, 2100], month [1, 12].
Entity list: min 1, unique entity_id per run.
No duplicate source snapshot references per entity in same run.
Positive tolerances required.
If rate_mode=month_end_locked, run fails validation when locked rate missing.
Test plan
Unit
FX application uses selected-rate path only; same-currency=1 handling.
Locked month-end enforcement behavior.
IC matcher classification (fx_explained, timing_difference, unexplained).
Elimination math and “no silent elimination” rule.
Aggregation totals and deterministic ordering.
FX delta formula correctness.
Excel deterministic byte/checksum for fixed fixture.
Integration
Endpoint auth and schema validation.
Temporal workflow executes and persists all tables.
AuditWriter invocation assertions on all write steps.
RLS runtime isolation for new consolidation tables.
Append-only trigger rejection for UPDATE and DELETE.
Correlation ID propagated to run + child rows + workflow payload.
Edge cases
One entity only.
All entities same currency.
Missing FX rate (month-end locked mode).
IC imbalance above tolerance.
Negative and zero amounts.
Zero-revenue entity.
DoD mapping
3 entities consolidated to USD correctly
Integration fixture with 3 entity currencies; verify consolidated parent totals and rate usage.
IC transaction eliminated
Matched IC pair test asserts elimination entry effect in consolidated totals.
Unexplained IC difference flagged
Over-tolerance IC pair test asserts classification unexplained, non-eliminated, surfaced via /ic-differences.
Parent-currency P&L exported
Export endpoint test validates sheet structure, deterministic ordering, and expected totals/FX impact columns.
Risks before implementation
Current source models (trial_balance_rows, gl_entries) do not consistently carry explicit IC counterparty metadata; matcher quality depends on normalized source conventions.
Strict month-end locked-rate enforcement may block runs until finance process has locked all required currency pairs.
Large consolidation payloads can pressure Temporal history/payload size if intermediate data is passed naively; implementation must keep payloads compact.
Append-only + status tracking requires careful run-state design (terminal row snapshots, no in-place mutation).
Excel determinism can drift if locale/formatting is not fixed explicitly (ordering, precision, date format).

What is good
1. Modular structure is correct
The split is healthy:

entity loading

FX application

IC matching

elimination

aggregation

FX impact

Excel export

thin facade

Temporal separated from core logic

That is exactly how this should be built.

2. FX dependency boundary is correct
Very important:

no raw provider reads

no reimplementation of FX

selected-rate path only

locked month-end behavior respected

That is the right dependency contract with Phase 1B.

3. “No silent elimination” is explicitly present
This is one of the most important finance controls in the whole platform.

If unexplained differences exist, they must be surfaced, not buried. Good.

4. Idempotency thinking is mature
The request signature + workflow ID approach is correct for consolidation.

Without that, reruns become messy very quickly.

5. Excel export is isolated
Also good.

Business logic should never leak into export generation.

What I want tightened before implementation
These are not rejections. They are design corrections to keep the module clean.

A. Be careful with consolidation_runs status design
You wrote:

Status is terminal snapshot row (no update-in-place).

Conceptually right, but practically this can become awkward if every state transition creates another run-like record.

I recommend this instead:

keep consolidation_runs as the immutable run header

keep a separate append-only consolidation_run_events table for lifecycle events:

accepted

running

completed

completed_with_unexplained

failed

That is cleaner than overloading the run header with multiple status snapshots.

If you do not want another table now, then at minimum define very clearly whether consolidation_runs is:

one row per run, or

one row per run-state snapshot.

Do not leave that ambiguous.

B. Clarify what “source_snapshot_reference” points to
Right now this is too abstract.

Before implementation, define whether it references:

TB rows

GL-derived normalized P&L snapshot

MIS upload snapshot

a generic source artifact key

For Phase 1C, I strongly recommend consuming a normalized P&L snapshot abstraction, not raw GL or raw TB directly. Otherwise consolidation logic gets polluted with source-format logic.

C. Tighten IC matching keys
You currently rely on:

ic_reference

account_code

entity pair

That is good, but in practice it may be too brittle.

I would add a matching strategy hierarchy:

explicit ic_reference

explicit counterparty metadata

fallback deterministic heuristics only if explicitly allowed

And Phase 1C should probably support only the first two. Do not let Codex build fuzzy matching too early.

D. Separate elimination results from source pairs
Your intercompany_pairs table includes classification and eliminated flag. Fine.

But make sure the actual elimination effect on the consolidated totals is traceable separately, either through:

elimination line items, or

explicit elimination result records.

Otherwise auditability gets weaker because the system knows a pair was eliminated, but not exactly what journal-style elimination effect was applied.

E. Define tolerance source
You mention:

positive tolerances required

Good, but tolerances must come from somewhere consistent.

Before implementation, define whether tolerance comes from:

request payload

tenant configuration

module default

account-specific rule later

For Phase 1C, I would use:

explicit request tolerance with sane default,

stored in run metadata for auditability.

F. Keep Temporal payloads light
You already noted payload-size risk. Good.

Implementation should pass:

run ID

tenant ID

source references

configuration

Not full line-item payloads between workflow stages.

That must be an explicit implementation rule.

My recommendation on data model
I would slightly refine your persistence plan like this:

Core:

consolidation_runs

consolidation_entities

consolidation_line_items

intercompany_pairs

consolidation_results

Strongly recommended add:

consolidation_run_events

consolidation_eliminations or equivalent trace table

That makes lifecycle and elimination trace cleaner.

Implementation caution
The biggest risk in Phase 1C is this:

Codex may try to put too much orchestration inside consolidation_service.py.

Do not allow that.

If consolidation_service.py starts becoming a god file, split immediately.

Verdict
Approved
This plan is good enough to implement.

This is a design clarification step only.
No code changes.

-------------------------------------------------------
1) CLARIFY RUN-STATUS PERSISTENCE PATTERN
-------------------------------------------------------

Current design states:
"consolidation_runs status is terminal snapshot row (no update-in-place)."

You must clarify precisely:

- Is consolidation_runs:
    A) one row per run (immutable header), or
    B) one row per run-state snapshot?

    - propose a separate append-only table: consolidation_run_events
    - define event types (accepted, running, completed, completed_with_unexplained, failed)
    - define how latest status is derived

If using snapshot-per-state:

    - explain how run identity is preserved
    - explain how queries retrieve current state cleanly

No ambiguity allowed.

-------------------------------------------------------

-------------------------------------------------------

"source_snapshot_reference"

You must define clearly:

    - MIS upload?
    - abstract financial artifact?

No fuzzy heuristics.

- Matching key priority order
- Required fields

    matched
    fx_explained
    timing_difference
    unexplained

-------------------------------------------------------
4) ADD EXPLICIT ELIMINATION TRACEABILITY
-------------------------------------------------------

Current design flags eliminated pairs but does not clearly persist elimination effect.

- How elimination impact is recorded.
Options:
    A) consolidation_eliminations table
    B) elimination line items inside consolidation_line_items
    C) explicit elimination result records

Design requirements:
- Traceable per IC pair
- Amount eliminated in parent currency
- FX component impact
- Correlation ID stored
- Append-only

Elimination must be auditable like a journal.
No silent adjustments.

-------------------------------------------------------
5) KEEP TEMPORAL PAYLOADS COMPACT AND DB-BACKED
-------------------------------------------------------

- What data is passed between workflow steps
- What is stored in DB between steps
- How workflow avoids passing full line-item arrays in payload

- Workflow payload must contain only:
    run_id
    tenant_id
    configuration
    lightweight metadata
- All heavy data read/written from DB inside activities

Explicitly describe:

- Activity boundaries
- What each activity loads from DB
- What each activity persists
- How idempotency is preserved

1) Finalized run-status persistence design

Finalized run-status persistence design
Chosen approach: Immutable run header + append-only run events.

consolidation_runs is one immutable row per logical run.
Run status transitions are stored in a new append-only table: consolidation_run_events.
consolidation_runs does not rely on mutable status; current status is always derived from events.
Tables

consolidation_runs

id (run_id), tenant_id, period_year, period_month, parent_currency

initiated_by, request_signature, configuration_json, workflow_id, correlation_id

created_at

Unique: (tenant_id, request_signature) for idempotent run creation

consolidation_run_events

id, tenant_id, run_id, event_seq, event_type, event_time

idempotency_key, metadata_json, correlation_id

event_type enum: accepted, running, completed, completed_with_unexplained, failed

Unique: (run_id, event_seq) and (run_id, event_type, idempotency_key)

Current status derivation

Latest status = event with highest event_seq for run_id.
Deterministic query rule: ORDER BY event_seq DESC LIMIT 1.
API /run/{id} returns latest derived status plus latest event metadata.
Idempotency

POST /run computes request_signature.
If (tenant_id, request_signature) exists, API returns existing run_id and latest derived status.
Workflow/activity retries use idempotency_key per event to avoid duplicate state events.
Source snapshot abstraction contract
Definition

A source snapshot is an immutable, tenant-scoped, normalized P&L artifact prepared upstream for consolidation consumption.
Phase 1C consumes only this contract, never raw GL/TB/MIS structures directly.

Snapshot contract (v1)

Header (normalized_financial_snapshots)

snapshot_id, tenant_id, entity_id, period_year, period_month

snapshot_type (fixed: normalized_pnl_v1)

entity_currency

produced_by_module (provenance)

source_artifact_reference (external/source link)

supersedes_snapshot_id (optional)

correlation_id, created_at

Lines (normalized_financial_snapshot_lines)

snapshot_line_id, tenant_id, snapshot_id

account_code

local_amount (signed decimal)

currency (ISO 4217)

ic_reference (optional)

counterparty_entity (optional)

transaction_date (optional)

ic_account_class (optional but required for Tier 2 IC match)

correlation_id, created_at

Immutability

Snapshot headers and lines are append-only.
Corrections create a new snapshot; old snapshot remains unchanged.
Consolidation run references explicit snapshot_ids only; no “latest snapshot” implicit lookup during execution.
How consolidation consumes it

Run request provides entity-to-snapshot mapping.
entity_loader validates snapshot ownership, period, entity uniqueness, and currency consistency.
Downstream activities read only snapshot IDs linked to the run.
RLS

Both snapshot tables are tenant-scoped with RLS policy on tenant_id.
Reads occur under session tenant context (app.current_tenant_id), so cross-tenant snapshot access is blocked at DB runtime.
Deterministic IC matching hierarchy
No fuzzy matching. No amount-based guessing. No AI.

Matching key priority order

Tier 1 key (highest priority)

ic_reference + ordered entity pair + account_code

Required: both lines have non-null ic_reference

Tier 2 key (fallback only if Tier 1 unavailable)

counterparty_entity + ordered entity pair + mirrored ic_account_class

Required: both lines have non-null counterparty_entity and ic_account_class

Mirrored class map (fixed in Phase 1C)

IC_RECEIVABLE ↔ IC_PAYABLE
IC_REVENUE ↔ IC_EXPENSE
If class is outside map, Tier 2 does not apply.

Classification rules (after deterministic pairing)

Let:

actual_difference = parent_amount_from + parent_amount_to
expected_difference = (local_amount_from * expected_rate_from) + (local_amount_to * expected_rate_to)
fx_variance = actual_difference - expected_difference
Then classify in this strict order:

matched if abs(actual_difference) <= amount_tolerance_parent
fx_explained if not matched and abs(fx_variance) <= fx_explained_tolerance_parent
timing_difference if above not met and both transaction_date present and abs(date_from - date_to) <= timing_tolerance_days
unexplained otherwise
Tolerance source

Request-level optional overrides:
amount_tolerance_parent
fx_explained_tolerance_parent
timing_tolerance_days
If omitted, resolved from tenant defaults.
Resolved values are persisted in immutable run configuration and used consistently across all activities.
Elimination traceability model
Chosen design: explicit append-only table consolidation_eliminations (Option A).

This is required for journal-like auditability.

id, tenant_id, run_id, intercompany_pair_id
entity_from, entity_to, account_code
classification_at_time (matched/fx_explained/timing_difference/unexplained)
elimination_status (applied/blocked)
eliminated_amount_parent
fx_component_impact_parent
residual_difference_parent
rule_code, reason
correlation_id, created_at
Rules

One elimination record per IC pair decision point.
applied only for allowed classes (matched, fx_explained).
blocked for timing_difference and unexplained with explicit reason and residual.
No silent adjustments; every elimination or non-elimination is persisted and auditable via AuditWriter.
Temporal payload and activity boundary design
Workflow payload must stay compact.

Workflow input payload

run_id
tenant_id
correlation_id
lightweight metadata only (for example config_hash, requested_by)
No line arrays, no snapshot contents, no IC pair lists in payload.

Activity boundaries

consolidation_mark_running_activity

Reads: run header/events

Writes: running run event

Idempotency: (run_id, event_type, idempotency_key)

consolidation_prepare_entities_activity

Reads: run config + snapshot headers

Writes: consolidation_entities

Idempotency: unique (run_id, entity_id)

consolidation_apply_fx_activity

Reads: consolidation_entities + snapshot lines + FX selected rates

Writes: consolidation_line_items

Idempotency: unique (run_id, snapshot_line_id)

consolidation_match_ic_activity

Reads: consolidation_line_items

Writes: intercompany_pairs

Idempotency: unique (run_id, match_key_hash)

consolidation_compute_eliminations_activity

Reads: intercompany_pairs

Writes: consolidation_eliminations

Idempotency: unique (run_id, intercompany_pair_id, elimination_status)

consolidation_aggregate_results_activity

Reads: line items + eliminations

Writes: consolidation_results

Idempotency: unique (run_id, consolidated_account_code)

consolidation_finalize_activity

Reads: results + ic pairs

Writes: terminal run event (completed, completed_with_unexplained, or failed) and export metadata reference

Idempotency: terminal event key fixed per run outcome

Failure handling

Any activity failure writes one failed event with structured error_code/error_detail.
Retries re-enter same run and skip already materialized stage outputs via idempotency constraints/checks.
Additional risk discovered during clarification
A normalized snapshot producer contract is now mandatory; if upstream modules do not emit normalized_pnl_v1, Phase 1C cannot run.
Tier 2 IC matching depends on ic_account_class; missing classification reduces matchability and may increase unexplained.
transaction_date optionality weakens deterministic timing classification; policy should define behavior when missing (default: cannot classify as timing_difference).
High volume runs require FX rate lookup batching/caching by (currency_pair, date, mode) to avoid excessive repeated selected-rate calls.
Explicit rerun semantics must be defined: same signature returns same run; changed inputs/tolerances must generate a new signature/run.

Excellent. This clarification is strong.

explicit elimination trace table

compact DB-backed Temporal activities

=======================================================
SERVICE MODULE RESPONSIBILITIES
=======================================================

entity_loader.py
- validate entity-to-snapshot mapping
- validate tenant ownership, entity uniqueness, period consistency
- load normalized snapshot headers and lines
- deterministic ordering by entity_id, account_code, reference

fx_application.py
- consume only Phase 1B selected-rate APIs/services
- same-currency conversion = 1
- month_end_locked mode requires locked selected rate presence
- daily mode uses selected daily rate by transaction_date
- no raw FX provider access

ic_matcher.py
- apply deterministic matching hierarchy only
- produce pair classification based on locked formula/order
- no fuzzy heuristics

elimination_engine.py
- create elimination decisions
- apply only for matched and fx_explained
- block timing_difference and unexplained
- persist explicit elimination trace

consolidation_aggregator.py
- aggregate parent totals by consolidated account code
- reflect elimination impacts
- produce consolidated parent totals and FX impact totals

fx_impact_calculator.py
- formula:
  fx_delta_component = parent_amount - (local_amount * expected_rate)
- quantization:
  rates 6dp
  persisted amounts 6dp
  output amounts 2dp

excel_exporter.py
- build deterministic Excel only
- no business logic
- stable ordering, precision, formatting, checksum

consolidation_service.py
- thin orchestration facade only
- use AuditWriter for all writes
- expose read methods for run status, results, ic-differences, export payload
- do not become a god file
